<div id="expander-1342159612" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1342159612" class="expand-control">

QA Automation Development

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1342159612" class="expand-content">

![](attachments/451825502/451825505.png)

![Related
image](https://miro.medium.com/max/2550/1*JOlEPRYTJQDdlniwWndeRA.png)

<div id="expander-1519378848" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1519378848" class="expand-control">

Develop

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1519378848" class="expand-content">

Code base will be deployed to Quality Assurance environment.

When testing has been signed off - it will be merged to Master.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1723604474" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1723604474" class="expand-control">

Master

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1723604474" class="expand-content">

Code base will be the template for "production"

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-493992016" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-493992016" class="expand-control">

Infrastructure As Code (IaC)

</div>

<div id="expander-content-493992016" class="expand-content">

![Related
image](https://miro.medium.com/max/2550/1*JOlEPRYTJQDdlniwWndeRA.png)

  

<div id="expander-1634746660" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1634746660" class="expand-control">

Develop

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1634746660" class="expand-content">

Code base will be deployed to Quality Assurance environment.

When testing has been signed off - it will be merged to Master.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1536021597" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1536021597" class="expand-control">

Master

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1536021597" class="expand-content">

Code base will be the template for "production"

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div class="panel" style="border-width: 1px;">

<div class="panelHeader" style="border-bottom-width: 1px;">

**To summarize Branches (charts above):**

</div>

<div class="panelContent">

<div id="expander-1315515121" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1315515121" class="expand-control">

Master

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1315515121" class="expand-content">

This branch represents the latest released / deployed code-base (In
Production).

This branch is only updated after a Hotfix or Release branch was
deployed to Production environment - NOT BEFORE.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-988575048" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-988575048" class="expand-control">

Develop

</div>

<div id="expander-content-988575048" class="expand-content">

This is your main development branch where all the changes destined for
the next release are placed, either by directly committing small changes
or by merging other branches (e.g. feature branches) into this branch.

Most Team City project will have a trigger off the Develop build
configuration. This package will only be deployed to Development
environments.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1205612017" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1205612017" class="expand-control">

Feature

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1205612017" class="expand-content">

Used to implement new features or fix a low priority defects in the
project. Features are designed to be created from the Develop branch,
and reintegrated in the Develop branch as well.

You can have many features as you want on a project; you'll see at usage
than working with too many features can be a nightmare

The name of the feature should have the Jira epic card number as
reference.

You will need to keep your feature branch up-to-date when other
developers updating the Develop branch. This will rebase your feature
branch on top of the develop.

When the feature has been finished, it must now be reviewed before being
merged. Refer to "Finishing Branch with SourceTree" below on how to do a
Pull Request.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-487303255" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-487303255" class="expand-control">

Hotfix

</div>

<div id="expander-content-487303255" class="expand-content">

Use to fix any defects or apply patch in production environment. Hotfix
is designed to be created off Master branch.

The name of the Hotfix should have the Jira card number as reference.

You will need to keep your Hotfix branch up-to-date when other
developers updating the Master branch.

When the Hotfix has been finished, it must now be reviewed before being
merged. Refer to "Finishing Branch with SourceTree" below on how to do a
Pull Request.

Once the package has been deployed to Production environment, merge the
branch to Master and Develop.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1886938230" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1886938230" class="expand-control">

Release

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1886938230" class="expand-content">

Release is designed to be created off Develop branch on promoting the
code to other environments.

Most Team City project will have a trigger off the Release build
configuration. This package will be deployed to CI (QA), Staging (UAT),
Production, and Disaster Recovery environments.

Once the package has been deployed to Production environment, merge the
branch to Master.

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div class="panel" style="border-width: 1px;">

<div class="panelHeader" style="border-bottom-width: 1px;">

**Git's Commit Process**

</div>

<div class="panelContent">

Regardless of the tool you use to interact with Git repository. The
commit workflow is as shown in the diagram below.

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*mQdKYVN0nl-NvTw6MOSkWA.png)

  - Workspace  - simply a set of git repositories on your local system
  - Index - The Git index is used as a staging area between your working
    directory and your repository. You can use the index to build up a
    set of changes that you want to commit together. When you create a
    commit, what is committed is what is currently in the index, not
    what is in your working directory.
  - Local Repository -
  - Remote Repository - "Bitbucket"

  

</div>

</div>

  

#### **Git Flow Way (Recommended)**

1.  Scenario 1 - Day to Day
    1.  Developers will create a Feature branch by using git flow.
    2.  When the developer is finished coding, finish branch (deal with
        conflict if any, commit and retry finishing again) this will
        auto merge back to Develop branches.
    3.  Create a Release branch by using git flow when ready to create
        deployments for QA/UAT/Prod.
    4.  After deployment to production and code-base is stable, finish
        the Release branch by using git flow, this will auto merge to
        Master and Develop.
2.  Scenario 2 - HotFix
    1.  Developer will create a Hotfix branch by using git flow.
    2.  When the developer is finished fix and is ready to deploy to
        production, merge Hotfix to Master and Develop branches.

#### **Manual Way**

1.  Scenario 1 - Day to Day
    1.  Developers will create a Feature branch off Develop or
        Development to work off.
    2.  When the developer is finished coding, ("get latest" and deal
        with conflict) merge back to Develop branch.
    3.  Create a Release branch when it is ready.
    4.  After deployment to production, merge Release branch to Master.
2.  Scenario 2 - HotFix
    1.  Developer will create a Hotfix branch off Master to work off
    2.  When the developer is finished and has been deployed to
        production, merge Hotfix to Master and Develop branches

  

  

  

#### Notes

1.  Always "get latest" to your branch before you merge. Good practice -
    every morning and before you commit update your branch.
2.  Pending on the source control Project/Repository there may be
    mandatory code review when doing a Pull Request.  Refer to the Pull
    Request section below
3.    

  

  

  

  

  

  

  

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-284111044" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-284111044" class="expand-control">

Git Data Transport Commands

</div>

<div id="expander-content-284111044" class="expand-content">

Author: [Ryan Kajiura](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/~ryank)

Git keeps copies of your source tree in the locations in this diagram .
(I’ll call these locations “data stores”.)

![](https://images.osteele.com/2008/git-transport.png)![](https://www.freecodecamp.org/news/content/images/2019/11/vcs.png)

  

The data store that’s new, relative to every other DVCS that I know
about, is the “index”. The one that’s new relative to centralized VCS’s
such as Subversion and Perforce is the “local repository”.

The illustration shows that `git add` is the only (everyday) operation
that can cause the index to diverge from the local repository. The only
reason (in Subversion-emulation mode) to use `git add` is so that `git
commit` will see your changes. The `-a`option to `git commit` causes
`git commit` to run `git add -u` first – in which case you never need
to`run git add -u` explicitly – in which case the index stays in sync
with the repository head. This is how the trick in “git without the
index” works: if you always use commit via `git commit -a`, you can
ignore the index.

  

So what’s the point of the index? Is it because Linus likes complicated
things? Is to one-up all the other repositories? Is it to increase the
complexity of system, so that you have a chance to shoot yourself in the
foot if you’re not an alpha enough geek?

  

Well, probably. But it’s good for something else as well. Several
things, actually; I’ll show you one (that I use), and point you to
another.

But first, a piece of background that helps in understanding Git. Git
isn’t at its core a VCS. It’s really a distributed versioning file
system, down to its
own[fsck](http://www.kernel.org/pub/software/scm/git/docs/git-fsck.html)and[gc](http://www.kernel.org/pub/software/scm/git/docs/git-gc.html).
It was developed as the bottom layer of a VCS, but the VCS layer, which
provides the conventional VCS commands (`commit`,`checkout`,`branch`),
is more like an uneven veneer than like the “porcelain” it’s sometimes
called: bits of file system (git core) internals poke through.

The disadvantage of this (leaky) layering is that Git is complicated. If
you look up how to diff against yesterday’s 1pm sources in[git
diff](http://www.kernel.org/pub/software/scm/git/docs/git-diff.html), it
will send you to[git
rev-parse](http://www.kernel.org/pub/software/scm/git/docs/git-rev-parse.html)from
the core; if you look up[git
checkout](http://www.kernel.org/pub/software/scm/git/docs/git-checkout.html),
you may end up
at[git-check-ref-format](http://www.kernel.org/pub/software/scm/git/docs/git-check-ref-format.html).
Most of this you can ignore, but it takes some reading to figure out
which.

The advantage of the layering is that you can use Git to build your own
workflows. Some of these workflows involve the index. Like the other
fancy Git features, building your own workflows is something that you
can ignore initially, and add when you get to where you need it. This
is, historically, how I’ve used the index: I ignored it until I was
comfortable with more of Git, and now I use it for a more productive
workflow than I had with other VCS’s. It’s not my main reason for using
Git, but it’s turned to a strength from being a liability.

  

  

  

  

  

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-14811552" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-14811552" class="expand-control">

Pull and Push Process

</div>

<div id="expander-content-14811552" class="expand-content">

Author: [Ryan Kajiura](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/~ryank)

  

![](https://www.freecodecamp.org/news/content/images/2019/11/explanation.png)

  

  

  

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div class="panel" style="border-width: 1px;">

<div class="panelHeader" style="border-bottom-width: 1px;">

**Data Model and Graph**

</div>

<div class="panelContent">

<div id="expander-17553534" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-17553534" class="expand-control">

Data Model

</div>

<div id="expander-content-17553534" class="expand-content">

Author: [Ryan Kajiura](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/~ryank)

This is why no matter how many git commands or tips ‘n tricks you
memorize or store in your git cheatsheet, without understanding of how
git works*under the hood*you will remain confused with the strange ways
of git because those git internals will every once in a while leak
through the abstraction layer git’s (frontend) commands give you.

So in this**Understanding Git**series we will cover git’s internals (we
will not go into git’s source code don’t worry) and first thing on that
list is git’s heart and soul — the**data model**.

To start, we will initialise and empty git repository in our project
directory:

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> git init
> ```

Git will inform us it has created a `.git`directory in our project’s
directory so let’s take a quick peak at how it looks like:

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> $ tree .git/
> ```
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--pre
> .git/
> ├── HEAD
> ├── config
> ├── description
> ├── hooks
> │   ├── applypatch-msg.sample
> │   ├── commit-msg.sample
> │   ├── post-update.sample
> │   ├── pre-applypatch.sample
> │   ├── pre-commit.sample
> │   ├── pre-push.sample
> │   ├── pre-rebase.sample
> │   ├── pre-receive.sample
> │   ├── prepare-commit-msg.sample
> │   └── update.sample
> ├── info
> │   └── exclude
> ├── objects
> │   ├── info
> │   └── pack
> └── refs
>     ├── heads
>     └── tags
> ```
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--pre
> 8 directories, 14 files
> ```

Some of these files and directories may sound familiar to you
(particularly`HEAD`) but for now we will focus on
the `.git/objects`directory which is empty right now, but we will
change that in a moment.

Let’s create an`index.php`file

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> touch index.php
> ```

give it some content

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> <?php
> echo "Hello World";
> ```

and a`README.md`file

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> touch README.md
> ```

and give it some content too:

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> # Description
> This is my hello world project
> ```

Now let’s stage and commit them:

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> git add .
> git commit -m "Initial Commit"
> ```

OK, nothing special here, adding and committing — we’ve all “been there,
done that”.

If we take a look again at our `.git`directory we can see that
the `.git/objects`directory has some subdirectories and files now:

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> ├── objects
> │   ├── 5d
> │   │   └── 92c127156d3d86b70ae41c73973434bf4bf341
> │   ├── a6
> │   │   └── dbf05551541dc86b7a49212b62cfe1e9bb14f2
> │   ├── cf
> │   │   └── 59e02c3d2a2413e2da9e535d3c116af1077906
> │   ├── f8
> │   │   └── 9e64bdfcc08a8b371ee76a74775cfe096655ce
> │   ├── info
> │   └── pack
> ```

*(Note: directories and files can/will have different names on you
computer)*

We will get back to `.git/objects`but for now notice that every
directory name is two characters long. Git generates a 40-character
checksum (SHA-1) hash for every object and the first two characters of
that checksum are used as directory name and the other 38 as file
(object) name.

The first kind of objects that git creates when we commit some file(s)
are**blob**objects, in our case two of them, one for each file we
committed:

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*grJYh5IvV9-ZfeHPxLzF0Q.png)

    Blob objects associated with our index.php and README.md files

They contain snapshots of our files (content of our files at the time of
the commit) and have their checksum header.

The next kind of objects git creates are**tree**objects. In our case
there is only one and it contains a list of all files in our project
with a pointer to the blob objects assigned to them (this is how git
associates your files with their blob objects):

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*sWI76t410z2Lh4ZfuAA30Q.png).

    Tree object pointing to blob objects

And finally git creates a**commit**object that has a pointer to it’s
tree object (along with some other information):

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*G2XdqybWn9KQTdH3QXGrIw.png)

    Commit object points to it’s tree object

If we look back at our `.git/objects`directory things should look
clearer now.

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> ├── objects
> │   ├── 5d
> │   │   └── 92c127156d3d86b70ae41c73973434bf4bf341
> │   ├── a6
> │   │   └── dbf05551541dc86b7a49212b62cfe1e9bb14f2
> │   ├── cf
> │   │   └── 59e02c3d2a2413e2da9e535d3c116af1077906
> │   ├── f8
> │   │   └── 9e64bdfcc08a8b371ee76a74775cfe096655ce
> │   ├── info
> │   └── pack
> ```

With`git log`we can see our commit history:

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> commit a6dbf05551541dc86b7a49212b62cfe1e9bb14f2 
> Author: zspajich <zspajich@gmail.com>
> Date:   Tue Jan 23 13:31:43 2018 +0100
> ```
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--pre
> Initial Commit
> ```

And using the naming convention we mentioned earlier we can find our
commit object in `.git/object` :

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> ├── objects
> │   ├── a6
> │   │   └── dbf05551541dc86b7a49212b62cfe1e9bb14f2
> ```

To look at it’s content we can’t simply use`cat`command since these are
not plain text files but git has a`cat-file`command we can use:

> git cat-file commit a6dbf05551541dc86b7a49212b62cfe1e9bb14f2

to get the content of our commit object:

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> tree f89e64bdfcc08a8b371ee76a74775cfe096655ce
> author zspajich <zspajich@gmail.com> 1516710703 +0100
> committer zspajich <zspajich@gmail.com> 1516710703 +0100
> ```
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--pre
> Initial Commit
> ```

Here we see the pointer to our commit’s tree object and to examine it’s
content we use`git ls-tree`command:

> git ls-tree f89e64bdfcc08a8b371ee76a74775cfe096655ce

and as expected it does contain a list of our files with pointers to
their blob objects:

> 
> 
> ``` graf graf--pre graf-after--p
> 100644 blob cf59e02c3d2a2413e2da9e535d3c116af1077906 README.md
> 100644 blob 5d92c127156d3d86b70ae41c73973434bf4bf341 index.php
> ```

We can look at blob object representing (for
example)`index.php`with`cat-file`command:  

> git cat-file blob 5d92c127156d3d86b70ae41c73973434bf4bf341

and we see that it contains our`index.php`file’s content

> \<?  
> echo "Hello World\!"

  

So that is what happens when we create and commit some files.

Now we’ll do another commit, this time let’s say we made some changes to
our`index.php`file (added some code magic) and commited those changes:

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*n5SQmlV-80O70sYxXUzZng.png)

    Git creates a new blob object for the file that has changed

As we see, git has now created a new blob object with a new snapshot
of`index.php`. Since`README.md`hasn’t changed, no new blob object for it
is created, git will reuse the existing one instead (we’ll see in a
second how).

Now, when git creates a tree**object, blob pointer assigned
to`index.php`is updated and blob pointer assigned to`README.md`simply
stays the same as in the previous commit’s tree.

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*O260Gac6Z03eVBnpajqOgA.png)

    Pointer to index.php blob is updated and pointer to README.md blob stays the same

And at the end, git creates a commit object with a pointer to it’s tree
object

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*7cO1qJI8GjAmxoIwuatEPg.png)

    Commit object points to it’s tree and also has a pointer to it’s parent commit object

and also a pointer to it’s parent*commit*object (every commit except the
first one has at least one parent).

So now that we know how git handles file adding and editing, the only
thing that remains is to see how it handles file deletion:

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*qRna_KHBVWGmcQTVuYIeaA.png)

    Git deletes the entry for index.php in tree object

It’s very simple — git deletes the file entry (file name with pointer to
it’s blob object) from the tree object. In this case we
deleted`index.php`in our commit so there is no longer an`index.php`entry
in that commit’s tree object (in other words, our commit’s tree object
no longer has a pointer to a blob object representing`index.php`).

There is just one more addition to this[data
model](https://hackernoon.com/tagged/data-model)we presented— tree
objects can be nested (they can point to other tree objects). You can
think of it this way: every blob object represents a file and every tree
object represents a directory, so if we have nested directories we will
have nested tree objects.

Let’s look at an example:

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*TYfg6X9HNjyk_6G06B3wMQ.png)

    Tree objects can point to other tree objects

Here, our project would have one`README.md`file and one app directory
with two files (`app.php`and`app_dev.php`).

Git uses blob objects to recreate the content of our files at any given
point in time (commit) and tree objects to reproduce our project’s
folder structure.

So there you have have it - git’s***data model***. It is in fact a
simple data model and
in[next](https://hackernoon.com/understanding-git-branching-2662f5882f9)post
we’ll look at branching and how git’s data model makes branching very
cheap and simple.

If you wish to dig deeper**into git’s data model I would recommend
this[lecture](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZDR433b0HJY)from Scott
Chacon and also going through[Git
Internals](https://git-scm.com/book/en/v2/Git-Internals-Plumbing-and-Porcelain)chapter
from his[Git Pro](https://git-scm.com/book/en/v2)book.

</div>

<div id="expander-content-186086974" class="expand-content">

  - [Hackernoon  - understanding git data
    model ](https://hackernoon.com/https-medium-com-zspajich-understanding-git-data-model-95eb16cc99f5)

<div id="expander-145771317" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-145771317" class="expand-control">

Graph

</div>

<div id="expander-content-145771317" class="expand-content">

Author: [Ryan Kajiura](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/~ryank)

  

Git represents history in a fundamentally different way than centralized
version controls systems (CVCS) such as Team Foundation Version Control,
Perforce, or Subversion. Centralized systems store a separate history
for each file in a repository.  Git stores history as a graph of
snapshots of the entire repository. These snapshots—which are called
commits in Git—can have multiple parents, creating a history that looks
like a graph instead of a straight line. This difference in history is
incredibly important and is the main reason users familiar with CVCS
find Git confusing.

![](https://hackernoon.com/hn-images/1*dY7Zq4fC4cVKdRDdiQ0QLg.png)

A core concept — once you can visualize it, you can troubleshoot and
recover from those weird Git situations you inevitably find yourself in
— is the way that commits relate to each other. And the wildly
accurate and incredibly unhelpful description of that way is “a directed
acyclic graph.” (Although, when I sat down to write this, my dashboard
contained a link to this cheerful deep-dive with illustrations:[Spinning
Around In Cycles With Directed Acyclic
Graphs](https://medium.com/basecs/spinning-around-in-cycles-with-directed-acyclic-graphs-a233496d4688).)

  

## Commit history basics

  

Start with a simple history example: a repo with 3 linear commits.

![three commits in a
line](https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/devops/learn/_img/history-abc-1.png)

Commit A is the parent of commit B, and commit B is the parent of commit
C. This history looks very similar to a CVCS. The arrow pointing to
commit C is a branch. It’s named master because that’s the default name
for the mainline branch in a Git repo. Branches are pointers to specific
commits, which is why branching is so lightweight and easy in Git.

A key difference in Git compared to CVCS is that I have my own full copy
of the repo. I need to keep my local repository in sync with the remote
repository by getting the latest commits from the remote repository. To
do this, I’ll pull the master branch with the following command:

> git pull origin master

This merges all changes from the master branch in the remote repository,
which Git names`origin`by default . This pull brought one new commit and
the master branch in my local repo moves to that commit.

![a fourth commit, D, is added to the
line](https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/devops/learn/_img/history-abcd-1.png)  

## Understand branch history

Now I want to make a change to my code. It’s common to have multiple
active branches where you’re working on different features in parallel.
This is in stark contrast to CVCS where new branches are heavy and
rarely created. The first step is to checkout to a new branch using the
following command:

>  git checkout -b cool-new-feature

This is a shortcut combining two commands:`git branch
cool-new-feature`to create the branch followed by`git checkout
cool-new-feature`to begin working in the branch.

![Branch cool-new-feature is
added](https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/devops/learn/_img/history-abcd-cool-new-feature-1.png)

Two branches now point to the same commit. I’ll make a few changes on
the`cool-new-feature`branch in two new commits, E and F.

![](https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/devops/learn/_img/history-abcd-cool-new-feature-e-f-1.png)

My commits are reachable by the`cool-new-feature`branch since I made
them in that branch. I’m done with my feature and want to merge it into
the master branch. To do that I’ll use the following command:

> `git merge cool-feature master`

![](https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/devops/learn/_img/history-abcd-cool-new-feature-e-f-merge-1.png)

The graph structure of history becomes visible when there’s a merge. Git
creates a new commit when I merged my branch into another branch. This
is a merge commit. There aren’t any changes included this merge commit
since I didn’t have conflicts. If I had conflicts, the merge commit
would include the changes needed to resolve those conflicts.

## History in the real world

Here is an example of Git history that more closely resembles code in
active development on a team. There are three people who merge commits
from their own branches into the master branch around the same time.

![console log of git
graph](https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/devops/learn/_img/gitlogconsole.png)

Now that you understand how branches and merges create the shape of the
graph, this shouldn’t be too scary\!

    history](https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/devops/learn/git/understand-git-history)

    that
    mean](https://medium.com/girl-writes-code/git-is-a-directed-acyclic-graph-and-what-the-heck-does-that-mean-b6c8dec65059)

Setting up Git Flow in Repository with SourceTree

</div>

<div id="expander-content-490381994" class="expand-content">

For every repository you will need to do this on your local system only
once

1.  Make sure you do not have anything files checked out

2.  Make sure you have <span style="color: rgb(0,128,0);">master and
    <span style="color: rgb(0,128,0);">develop (or
    <span style="color: rgb(0,128,0);">development) branch checked out
    under <span style="color: rgb(0,128,0);">BRANCHES  
    </span></span></span></span>
    
    ![](attachments/423266600/423268241.png)

3.  If you do not have those branches checked out  
    
    1.  Check to see if you have master under Branches. If you do not
        like this screen shot. Expand Remotes \> origin  
        ![](attachments/423266600/423268239.png)  
    2.  Double click master. You will see this dialog, just click OK.   
          
        ![](attachments/423266600/423268240.png)  
          

4.  Click on the Git-flow  
      
    ![](attachments/423266600/423267576.png)  
      

5.  Use the default  
      
    ![](attachments/423266600/423267580.png)

<div id="expander-582331927" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-582331927" class="expand-control">

Create Branch From Jira Ticket

</div>

<div id="expander-content-582331927" class="expand-content">

  

1.  From within the ticket, click on the Create Branch link on the right
    hand side under the Development subheading. This will redirects you
    to Bitbucket.  
    ![](attachments/423266600/423267379.png)
2.  Select the 'Your-website Repository' (e.g. ITIS Web Repository) and
    Branch Type (e.g. **Feature**); confirm that the ‘Branch from’ value
    is ‘**develop**’ and change the Branch name if necessary (it
    auto-fills with the JIRA ticket subject line). Click Create
    Branch.  
    ![](attachments/423266600/423268034.png)  
      
3.  Return to SourceTree and perform a Fetch operation (see Step 1. -
    [Web Editing with
    SourceTree/Git/Stash](https://my.usgs.gov/confluence/pages/viewpage.action?pageId=537496521)).
    The new Branch Type Feature folder now appears under the Remotes
    menu in the repository column (because we selected Feature (as
    opposed to Bugfix) as the Branch Type).  
    ![](attachments/423266600/423268036.png)  
      
4.  Right-click on new branch under the Feature folder and select
    Checkout origin/feature/name of your new branch from the menu.  
    ![](https://my.usgs.gov/confluence/download/attachments/537496516/image2015-7-28%209%3A50%3A3.png?version=1&modificationDate=1438098714158&api=v2)  
      
5.  Right-click on new branch under the Feature folder and select
    Checkout origin/feature/name of your new branch from the menu.  
    ![](https://my.usgs.gov/confluence/download/attachments/537496516/image2015-7-28%209%3A51%3A33.png?version=1&modificationDate=1438098714181&api=v2)  
      
6.  You’re now ready to start coding. After you've completed your
    changes, follow the Commit and Pull Request instructions as per
    usual.

</div>

**Linking Jira User Story to Git Commits**

</div>

Jira Smart Commits

</div>

<div id="expander-content-384732699" class="expand-content">

Author: [Ryan Kajiura](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/~ryank)

  

JIRA and Bitbucket has been configured to handle Smart Commits. This
feature is available in all JIRA projects. Only JIRA users that are in
either permissions groups IT Dev Team Leads or IT Developers.

Other option is to create a branch through JIRA

When commit changes to source control, follow this format. 

<div class="table-wrap">

| Description | Developers comment                  |
| ----------- | ----------------------------------- |
| Syntax      | \<ISSUE\_KEY\> : \<Comments\>       |
| Example     | CUS-222: Add XML Comments to method |

</div>

Screenshots will show what will happen when you follow this comment
format.

SourceTree commits to a JIRA ticket.

![](attachments/416088562/416088788.png)

When you open the JIRA ticket, you will see the Development panel

![](attachments/416088562/416088789.png)

When you click on the commits link (above screenshot), you will see all
of the commits that are associated to this user story (see yellow
highlighted section). 

When clicking on any hyperlink under the Commit column - it will
redirect you to Bitbucket file compare.

When clicking on any hyperlink under File column - it will show you the
files that was committed.

![](attachments/416088562/416088790.png)

<div id="expander-1573625632" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1573625632" class="expand-control">

Comments Standard

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1573625632" class="expand-content">

Author: [Ryan Kajiura](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/~ryank)

## Why should you write good commit messages?

You might say, "It's just a personal project," but yes, you work alone
now, but what happens when you work with a team or contribute to open
source?

A well-crafted commit message is the best way to communicate context
about a change to other developers working on that project and indeed to
your future self. Have you tried running history on your old projects to
see the "weird" commit messages you have used since inception? It
becomes hard for you to explain why you made some changes in the past,
and you wish you have read this article before now :).

Commit messages can adequately tell you why a change was made, and
understanding why a change was made makes development and collaboration
more efficient.

## How to write good commit messages

There are several conventions used by several teams and developers to
write good commit messages. I'll only outline some general rules and
tips for writing commit messages, you would have to decide what
convention you want to follow and if you work for a company or
contribute to open source, you would have to adapt to their convention
:).

For consistency, you can use one convention for work and another for
personal projects as you might change your job sometime, and the
convention might change.

> 
> 
> ``` language-bash
> Capitalized, short (50 chars or less) summary
> 
> More detailed explanatory text, if necessary.  Wrap it to about 72
> characters or so.  In some contexts, the first line is treated as the
> subject of an email and the rest of the text as the body.  The blank
> line separating the summary from the body is critical (unless you omit
> the body entirely); tools like rebase can get confused if you run the
> two together.
> 
> Write your commit message in the imperative: "Fix bug" and not "Fixed bug"
> or "Fixes bug."  This convention matches up with commit messages generated
> by commands like git merge and git revert.
> 
> Further paragraphs come after blank lines.
> 
> - Bullet points are okay, too
> 
> - Typically a hyphen or asterisk is used for the bullet, followed by a
>   single space, with blank lines in between, but conventions vary here
> 
> - Use a hanging indent
> 
> If you use an issue tracker, add a reference(s) to them at the bottom,
> like so:
> 
> Resolves: #123
> ```

Looks great, right? Here's how you can make yours great too:

1.  Specify the type of commit:

<!-- end list -->

  - **feat**:  The new feature you're adding to a particular application
  - **fix**: A bug fix
  - **style**: Feature and updates related to styling
  - **refactor**: Refactoring a specific section of the codebase
  - **test**: Everything related to testing
  - **docs**: Everything related to documentation
  - **chore**: Regular code maintenance.  
    \[ You can also use emojis to represent commit types\]

<!-- end list -->

1.  Separate the subject from the body with a blank line
2.  Your commit message should not contain any whitespace errors
3.  Remove unnecessary punctuation marks
4.  Do not end the subject line with a period
5.  Capitalize the subject line and each paragraph
6.  Use the imperative mood in the subject line
7.  Use the body to explain what changes you have made and why you made
    them.
8.  Do not assume the reviewer understands what the original problem
    was, ensure you add it.
9.  Do not think your code is self-explanatory
10. Follow the commit convention defined by your team

  

  

<div id="expander-1638434016" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1638434016" class="expand-control">

References

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1638434016" class="expand-content">

  - [Free code camp - writing good commit messages a practical
    guide](https://www.freecodecamp.org/news/writing-good-commit-messages-a-practical-guide/)
  - [Github -  Git Commit Best
    Practices](https://github.com/trein/dev-best-practices/wiki/Git-Commit-Best-Practices)
  - [Medium - Git commit best
    practices](https://medium.com/@nawarpianist/git-commit-best-practices-dab8d722de99)
  - [Tim Pope - A note about git commit
    messages](https://tbaggery.com/2008/04/19/a-note-about-git-commit-messages.html)

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div class="panel" style="border-width: 1px;">

<div class="panelHeader" style="border-bottom-width: 1px;">

**Conflicts**

</div>

<div class="panelContent">

<div id="expander-1076229166" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1076229166" class="expand-control">

Resolve Conflicts

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1076229166" class="expand-content">

[Git - Resolve
Conflicts](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/IT/Git+-+Resolve+Conflicts)

Document contributed by [Ryan
Kajiura](Git---Resolve-Conflicts_419988568.html)

  

When you create a pull request Bitbucket automatically compares the
source with your update and the destination with the original code. If
anyone else has made changes in the destination to the same code you
touched, we'll notify you of conflicts when you attempt to merge.

![](attachments/419988568/419988570.png)

When you have merge conflicts, you can't click the **Merge** button from
the pull request to merge. To resolve these conflicts, you pull the
changes to your local repository and fix them there. Merge conflicts may
look something like this:

  

![](attachments/419988568/419988571.png)

  

## Resolving the conflict between Git branches

These steps include details for resolving conflicts between two branches
in a Git repository. You'll see references to these placeholders:

  - The directory to the forked repository as `<repo_directory>`
  - The destination branch as `<destination_branch>`
  - The source branch as `<feature_branch>`
  - The file with resolved conflicts as `<filename>`

  

## Resolving the conflict between Git forks

These steps include details for resolving conflicts between a forked
repository and its original Git repository. You'll see references to
these placeholders:

  - The directory to the forked repository as `<repo_directory>`
  - The URL of the original repository as `<original_repo_URL>`
  - The file with resolved conflicts as `<filename>`
  - The destination branch of the forked repository
    as `<destination_branch>`

<div id="expander-714444371" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-714444371" class="expand-control">

Pull Request Process

</div>

<div id="expander-content-714444371" class="expand-content">

[Git - Work with Pull
Requests](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/IT/Git+-+Work+with+Pull+Requests)

Author: [Ryan Kajiura](https://wiki.pinnacle.com/display/~ryank)

*Pull requests are an effective way to perform code review and
collaboration in a positive way in the team.*

## Pull requests offer

  - Complete diff (difference) of the changes to each file.
  - Automatic updates so you can see changes as they are made
  - Inline comments so you can pinpoint change suggestions
  - Tasks to help you keep track of what changes need attention
  - Notifications for comments, commits, or approvals
  - Two merge strategy options for Git repositories

<div class="panel" style="border-width: 1px;">

<div class="panelHeader" style="border-bottom-width: 1px;">

**Pull Request Process**

</div>

<div class="panelContent">

<div id="expander-1933661904" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1933661904" class="expand-control">

Build better Pull Requests

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1933661904" class="expand-content">

Here are a few suggestions to make PULL REQUESTS more effective.

  - Try to make the pull request small enough to review but large enough
    to give you the context of the feature, bug fix, or update
  - Use @mentions  and in-line comments to guide specific reviewers to
    specific items in the code
  - Ask reviewers to create TASKS for things which must be addressed for
    APPROVAL. This helps designate between suggestions and discussions
    and things which must be fixed
  - An accurate description and links to any issues gives reviewers more
    context and better access to what they need to understand the
    problem or feature you're addressing

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1332759844" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1332759844" class="expand-control">

Create a Pull Request

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1332759844" class="expand-content">

### Option 1: Full Stash interface

1.  ACTIONS (left side menu) \> Click CREATE PULL REQUEST  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735567.png)

2.  (**A**) Select Branch: Select the branch with the changes you want
    to merge

3.  (**B**) Select Source and Destination: From the drop down, check the
    destination repository and branch

4.  (**C**) Click CONTINUE  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735568.png)

5.  (**D**) TITLE: Add a Title that is appropriate

6.  (**E**) DESCRIPTION: Add a Description that can be easily recognized
    (in notifications and the pull request list)

7.  (**F**) REVIEWERS: Start to type the reviewer's name and select it
    from the list provided in the automatic search

8.  (**G**) Click CREATE  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735569.png)

**<span class="underline">Note</span>**

Select reviewers for this pull request to make key contributors aware of
the changes and create an effective review.

Every REVIEWER can COMMENT on the pull request, and with a single click
give their APPROVAL.

If changes are made to the code, they can see those changes as soon as
the new commit is made.

Additionally, the contributors you invite can decide to stop watching
the pull request with a simple click.

### Option 2: SourceTree

When you commit your feature branch to remote

Right click on the feature branch and click Create pull request....

![](attachments/405735527/426476109.png)

This will redirect you to Stash interface

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1420814182" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1420814182" class="expand-control">

Add Reviewers to your Pull Request

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1420814182" class="expand-content">

1\. Add a REVIEWER to the Pull Request when you create it by **(F1)**
typing their Bitbucket USER or EMAIL to the Reviewers section of the
page.

2\. And then click to **(F2)** select the desired name from the
automatic list of users.

![](attachments/405735527/405735579.png)  

3\. After you click to select the desired name, it will **(F3)**
automatically populate as one of the REVIEWERS.

![](attachments/405735527/405735580.png)

4\. Add more reviewer names as desired.

Note: You may also add REVIEWERS to the Pull Request after it is in
progress by clicking EDIT button (between *Merge* and *Decline*) at the
top of the request.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-660555528" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-660555528" class="expand-control">

Compare before issuing a Request

</div>

<div id="expander-content-660555528" class="expand-content">

Before creating a Pull Request, you should compare your outgoing
Requests to the destination Repository.This tells you how much the
destination Repository changed while you were working on your fork or
branch. You do this by comparing incoming Changes.

→ It is good practice to make sure that there are no incoming changes
before you make your Pull Request.

If there are changes what do you do? It depends on your project
workflow. For example, if you are working on a team, your project
workflow might require you to MERGE and TEST incoming changes *before*
sending your outgoing changes back to the destination.

#### To compare your source to the destination, do the following.

1.  From the Repository with the changes, **(H1)** click  **...**  (top
    center) and **(H2)** select COMPARE  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735627.png)  
    Bitbucket displays**(H3)** the *Compare *page.  
      
2.  Adjust the SOURCE and DESTINATION values so that they match the Pull
    Request you anticipate making.  
      
    a) Click to access**(H4)** the DIFF tab to see **(H5)** "There are
    no changes" (if there were no changes).  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735607.png)  
      
    b) Click to access**(H6)** the COMMITS tab to see **(H7)** "There
    are no commits found" (if there were no commits)  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735608.png)  
      
    c) Note:  If there are changes, you'll see a DIFF of the changes and
    a list of COMMITS.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1609260234" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1609260234" class="expand-control">

Request the destination Owner pull your changes

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1609260234" class="expand-content">

**To create a Pull Request, do the following:**

1.  From the repository, in the ACTIONS sidebar select CREATE PULL
    REQUEST  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735863.png)  
    Bitbucket displays the **(I1)** PULL REQUEST page.
2.  Use the drop down to **(I2)** Select Branch.
3.  Press **(I3)** CONTINUE. Note: The "Continue" button will not become
    available until you "Select Branch".  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735873.png)
4.  Enter a **(I4)** TITLE and **(I5)** DESCRIPTION for your Pull
    Request.
5.  Add a **(I6)** REVIEWER
6.  Click **(I7)** CREATE  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405735875.png)
7.  The Pull Request is now created.

Note: Immediately in EMAIL and in STASH, the Reviewer will receive
NOTIFICATION that there is a new Pull Request.

To see the list of all the Pull Requests against this repository, you
can click **(I8)** PULL REQUESTS in the Navigation bar.

![](attachments/405735527/405735874.png)

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-515467193" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-515467193" class="expand-control">

Check Build Status on a Pull Request

</div>

<div id="expander-content-515467193" class="expand-content">

If your vendor or build system runs a Build when you make a Commit, you
can see the Status of the Build(s) on each Commit.

After you create a Pull Request, its OVERVIEW tab includes the Build
Status for all the Commits on that Pull Request.

Click the COMMITS tab to see the Builds Statuses separated by Commits.

![](attachments/405735527/405736258.png)

The BUILDS column contains one of these statuses:

  - ![](https://confluence.atlassian.com/bitbucket/files/304578655/790630066/1/1447862298449/build_status_passed.png)appears
    if all builds for that commit passed  
  - ![](https://confluence.atlassian.com/bitbucket/files/304578655/790630067/1/1447862317670/build_status_progress.png)appears
    if no builds for that commit failed and at least one build is in
    progress  
  - ![](https://confluence.atlassian.com/bitbucket/files/304578655/790630068/1/1447862335598/build_status_failed.png)appears
    if at least one build for that commit failed  

From the COMMIT column, click the link to the Commit that you want to
see. 

On the right side of the COMMIT page, you see the BUILD STATUS icon
again and a link to MORE DETAILS.

  

Click the link to see MORE DETAILS of each Build for the Commit. 

The TABLE that opens when you click a BUILD STATUS includes only the
most recent status for each BUILD, not a History of Builds for the
Commit.

You can also check the BUILD STATUSES for a repository without creating
a PULL REQUEST.

To see the BUILD STATUS on a Commit, press COMMITS on the left side of
the repository.

![](attachments/405735527/405738292.png)

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1323275995" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1323275995" class="expand-control">

Accept a Pull Request

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1323275995" class="expand-content">

Only a user with write permissions on the destination repository can
ACCEPT or REJECT a Pull Request.

Any user with read permission in a repository can REVIEW the list of
open, accepted and rejected Pull Requests. 

**The following procedure shows the steps to ACCEPT a Pull Request:**

1.  From your repository, click PULL REQUESTS in the sidebar.  
    A list of incoming Pull Requests display with the OPEN context. 
2.  On the list, click a Pull Request.  
    The Pull Request DETAILS appear and the VIEW defaults to the
    DIFF context.  
    You can also view the COMMITS or the Comment Activity on the
    request.  
    ![](attachments/405735527/405736259.png)
3.  Examine the request using the various contexts..
4.  If you decide that you want to Merge the Fork into your own
    repository, click MERGE.
5.  Confirm the action if prompted.   
    Bitbucket merges the changes into your repository, all on the
    Bitbucket server.

<div class="confluence-information-macro confluence-information-macro-information">

<span class="aui-icon aui-icon-small aui-iconfont-info confluence-information-macro-icon"></span>

<div class="confluence-information-macro-body">

If an UPDATE PULL REQUEST button appears by a request, this means that
the fork changed after the Pull Request was sent.

You can click UPDATE PULL REQUEST to apply those changes to the Pull
Request.

Or, you can ACCEPT and PULL what is in the Request alone.

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-510128025" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-510128025" class="expand-control">

Merge a Pull Request

</div>

<div id="expander-content-510128025" class="expand-content">

Merging your changes is the final stage of the pull request process.

**Complete the following steps to merge a pull request:**

1.  Click the MERGE button.

You'll see something similar to the following screenshot.

![](attachments/405735527/405736263.png)

2\. (Optional) Update the COMMIT MESSAGE with more details.

3\. (For Git repositories only) Select a MERGE STRATEGY from the two
options:

  - MERGE COMMIT - Keeps all commits from your source branch and makes
    them part of the destination branch.  
    This option is the same as entering  **`git merge --no-ff`** in the
    command line.  
      
  - SQUASH - Combines your commits when you merge the source branch into
    the destination branch.  
    This option is the same as entering  **`git merge --squash`**  in
    the command line.  
      
    *→ For more details on these two types of merge strategies, refer to
    the rest of this section.*  

4\. (Optional) If you're merging two branches in the same repository,
you can select the CLOSE SOURCE BRANCH checkbox to remove the branch
from the list of repository branches.

5\. Click the MERGE button.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-2016651550" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-2016651550" class="expand-control">

Merge Checklist

</div>

<div id="expander-content-2016651550" class="expand-content">

As you're about to Merge your Pull Request, you may see a MERGE
CHECKLIST

This Checklist is a list of items that your administrator requests that
you resolve *before you merge your code*.

Any checklist items with a
![(warning)](images/icons/emoticons/warning.png) are unresolved.

If your administrator requires that you resolve the items, you won't be
able to merge.

Otherwise, you can choose to ignore these warnings on the checklist and
merge anyway.

  

<div class="table-wrap">

| If this checklist item has a ![(warning)](images/icons/emoticons/warning.png) | Then your pull request...                                                |
| ----------------------------------------------------------------------------- | ------------------------------------------------------------------------ |
| **At least {\#} approvals**                                                   | doesn't have that number of approvals                                    |
| **At least {\#} successful builds on last commit**                            | doesn't have that number of successful builds for the most recent commit |
| **No failed builds on last commit**                                           | has failed builds on the most recent commit                              |
| **Resolved pull request tasks**                                               | has open pull request tasks                                              |

</div>

  

![](attachments/405735527/405736231.png)

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-233345257" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-233345257" class="expand-control">

Merge Strategies

</div>

<div id="expander-content-233345257" class="expand-content">

<div class="confluence-information-macro confluence-information-macro-information">

<span class="aui-icon aui-icon-small aui-iconfont-info confluence-information-macro-icon"></span>

<div class="confluence-information-macro-body">

While merge strategies currently only apply to Git repositories, we're
planning to support them on Mercurial repositories in the future.

At the moment, clicking MERGE on a Mercurial pull request performs a
MERGE COMMIT.

</div>

</div>

Both strategies merge all your commits from your source branch to the
destination branch.

The type of strategy you chose to use depends on your team's workflow
and how you use your repository.

#### Strategy 1: When to do a Merge Commit

Select MERGE COMMIT when you want to maintain an exact history of
changes. Merge commits are also useful if, as part of your workflow,
pull requests are large in scope and you review commits
individually. Because this strategy keeps all commits during the merge,
you'll still see all commits from the source branch on the COMMITS page.

#### Strategy 2: When to do a Squash Merge

Select SQUASH to make your commits list less cluttered, which results
in less time to search for commits that introduce a bug (with a `git
bisect`) and provides an easy-to-follow commit history. Because this
strategy combines all commits when you merge, you'll only see one commit
on the destination branch on the COMMITS page.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-579771341" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-579771341" class="expand-control">

Revert a Pull Request

</div>

<div id="expander-content-579771341" class="expand-content">

While you can't undo a pull request, you can revert the merged commit
from a pull request if necessary.

Bitbucket takes two steps to revert a pull request:

1\) Bitbucket creates a new branch with one commit that reverses the
merged commit.

2) Bitbucket creates a new pull request for the branch and commit.

  

**To Revert a Pull Request**

1.  From the pull request, click the REVERT button in the top right.

2.  (Optional) From the REVERT PULL REQUEST dialog, change the BRANCH
    NAME for the new branch you're about to create.

3.  Click the REVERT button.  
    **NOTE**:  Once you click REVERT, Bitbucket creates the new branch.
    Even if you CANCEL the pull request, the revert branch remains in
    the repository.

4.  The CREATE A PULL REQUEST page opens with the revert branch as the
    source.  
    After you add your reviewers and make additional changes, click
    CREATE.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-986872896" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-986872896" class="expand-control">

Decline a Pull Request

</div>

<div id="expander-content-986872896" class="expand-content">

Declining a pull request is the same as saying you don't want to ever
merge the changes in that pull request in their current state.

Any reviewer can decline a pull request so your team might want to
establish a policy about when to decline verses when to leave the pull
request open in a stagnant state.

**NOTE**: Declining a pull request cannot be undone. Once you decline a
pull request you will have to open a new pull request request to
continue a review. Declining a pull request has no effect on the
branches (source or destination) so the changes in the source branch are
still in that source branch.

To decline a pull request, navigate to the Pull Request and click the
DECLINE button (top left of the page).

**To view a declined pull request**

1.  From your repository, click PULL REQUESTS (in the sidebar).
2.  At the top, select the third tab DECLINED
3.  Select the desired Declined Pull Request

The comments and tasks in a pull request are preserved after it is
declined. 

You can add more comments to a declined pull request, however, new
changes pushed to the branch will no longer update the pull request.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-191318113" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-191318113" class="expand-control">

Resolve a Pull Request with Conflicts

</div>

<div id="expander-content-191318113" class="expand-content">

When it detects Conflicts in a pull request's incoming code, Bitbucket
cannot automatically Accept and Merge.

If this happens, Bitbucket notifies you and instructs you how to
proceed when you click MERGE.

![](attachments/405735527/405736268.png)

1.  **Source and destination:** The source repository and branch name
    points to the destination branch name.
2.  **Conflict instructions:** Use these instructions to merge locally. 

To resolve these kinds of conflicts, you pull the changes to your local
repository and resolve them there.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-527328259" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-527328259" class="expand-control">

Manually Merge Pull Requests locally

</div>

<div id="expander-content-527328259" class="expand-content">

Sometimes, it is a good idea to use a workflow where you test a change
set on your local system before accepting a pull request.

You can do this with any pull request.

**The typical workflow is this**.

  - Receive a pull request in Bitbucket
  - Update your local repository with the incoming change set
  - Investigate and/or test the change set

→If you the change set is good, you merge it into your local repository.

You may have to resolve some conflicts. Then, you push the local
repository back to Bitbucket. Back on Bitbucket, the pull request is
marked as accepted in the PULL REQUESTS tab.

→ If you don't like the change request, you discard the changes locally
and reject the pull request on Bitbucket.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1447219508" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1447219508" class="expand-control">

Git Command Line example

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1447219508" class="expand-content">

<div class="table-wrap">

<table>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><p>This is a simple Git example of the procedure for pulling changes made by another user from a fork of a Bitbucket repository, back into the original repository also on Bitbucket.</p>
<ol>
<li><p>Check for incoming changes <em>(one change detected)</em></p>
<div class="table-wrap">
<table>
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><code>$ git fetch &amp;&amp; git log ..origin/master</code><code></code>
<pre><code>Password:
commit 2f41d64e75f2b4f0405cbfb1d2f882882809c209
Author: ANDREW &lt;alui@atlassian.com&gt;
Date: Thu Sep 8 11:50:08 2011 +1000

 adding images</code></pre></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></li>
<li><p>Check for outgoing changes <em>(no changes detected)</em>.</p>
<div class="table-wrap">
<table>
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><code>$ git fetch &amp;&amp; git log origin/master..</code>
<pre><code>Password:
remote: Counting objects: 6, done.
remote: Compressing objects: 100% (3/3), done.
remote: Total 4 (delta 1), reused 0 (delta 0)
Unpacking objects: 100% (4/4), done.
From https://staging.bitbucket.org/alui/alui-git
 2f41d64..7e28ddb master -&gt; origin/master</code></pre></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div>
<p>3. Pull the changes into the local repository.</p>
<div class="table-wrap">
<table>
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><code>$ git pull</code>
<pre><code>Password:
Updating 2f41d64..7e28ddb
Fast-forward
 Thumbs.db | Bin 0 -&gt; 11776 bytes
 file.txt | 4 +++-
 2 files changed, 3 insertions(+), 1 deletions(-)
 create mode 100644 Thumbs.db</code></pre></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></li>
</ol></td>
</tr>

<div id="expander-1556466123" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1556466123" class="expand-control">

Mercurial Command Line example

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1556466123" class="expand-content">

<div class="table-wrap">

<table>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><p>This is a simple Mercurial example of the procedure for pulling changes made by another user from the main Bitbucket repository, down into the local working copy.</p>
<ol>
<li><p>Check for incoming changes <em>(one change detected)</em>.</p>
<div class="table-wrap">
<table>
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><code>$ hg incoming https://bitbucket.org/sarahcm/sarahmaddox</code>
<pre><code>comparing with https://bitbucket.org/sarahcm/sarahmaddox
searching for changes
changeset:   6:9f0a3d22d0be
tag:         tip
user:        Sarah Maddox &lt;smaddox@atlassian.com&gt;
date:        Fri Aug 27 12:07:21 2010 +1000
summary:     Changed &#39;bounding&#39; to &#39;pounding&#39; and added pic of cockatoo&#39;</code></pre></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></li>
<li><p>Check for outgoing changes <em>(no changes detected)</em>.</p>
<div class="table-wrap">
<table>
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><code>$ hg outgoing https://bitbucket.org/sarahcm/sarahmaddox</code>
<pre><code>comparing with https://bitbucket.org/sarahcm/sarahmaddox
searching for changes
no changes found</code></pre></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></li>
<li><p>Pull the changes into the local repository.</p>
<div class="table-wrap">
<table>
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><code>$ hg pull https://bitbucket.org/sarahcm/sarahmaddox</code>
<pre><code>pulling from https://bitbucket.org/sarahcm/sarahmaddox
searching for changes
adding changesets
adding manifests
adding file changes
added 1 changesets with 2 changes to 2 files
(run &#39;hg update&#39; to get a working copy)</code></pre></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div>
<p><br />
</p></li>
<li><p>Update the working copy.</p>
<div class="table-wrap">
<table>
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><code>$ hg update</code>
<pre><code>2 files updated, 0 files merged, 0 files removed, 0 files unresolved</code></pre></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></li>
<li><p>Push the changes up to Bitbucket.</p>
<div class="table-wrap">
<table>
<colgroup>
<col style="width: 100%" />
</colgroup>
<tbody>
<tr class="odd">
<td><code>$ hg push</code>
<pre><code>pushing to http://bitbucket.org/sarahmaddox/sarahmaddox
searching for changes
http authorization required
realm: bitbucket.org HTTP
user: sarahmaddox
password:
remote: adding changesets
remote: adding manifests
remote: adding file changes
remote: added 1 changesets with 2 changes to 2 file</code></pre></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>
</div></li>
</ol></td>
</tr>
</tbody>
</table>

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

  

<div class="panel" style="border-width: 1px;">

<div class="panelHeader" style="border-bottom-width: 1px;">

**Pull Request Process**

</div>

<div class="panelContent">

<div id="expander-750475006" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-750475006" class="expand-control">

Clear and concise title

</div>

<div id="expander-content-750475006" class="expand-content">

Code reviewers will skim the title of the change to decide if they want
to review the code and if so, when. So let's make this easy for them.

**Keep the title short, but expressive enough to signal what the
reviewer can expect.**Let's say you're working on an accounting software
and there is a new business requirement on how to calculate tax for
Switzerland. A title like`Changes for Swiss tax calculation`is concise.
A title like`Change for tax calculation in Switzerland for new
regulation`is still concise, with more context. However, a title
like`Adding new tax parameters`or`Change tax logic`are overly generic
and won't help reviewers give an idea of the type of change. This will
make it difficult for them to decide if they should review the change.
What about titles like`TaxInternals structure update and
CalculateEffectiveRate changes in case of Swiss country code` or `Change
tax calculation for Swiss businesses in the TX bracket effective
from 2019 following new regulation`? These are on the other end of the
spectrum: they are verbose and feel they provide like too much detail in
the title. What is too little and what is too much information will
depend on your environment: try to strike the right balance.

As the team grows, it can make sense to agree on conventions to make
titles easy to compare. One practice, for example, that my team have
adopted is starting the title with uppercase and the first word being a
verb in present tense. So we prefer`Change Swiss tax calculation for new
regulation`over`changed Swiss tax calculation for new
regulation` or `Ability to calculate Swiss tax for new regulation`.
This kind of suggestion can be a nice touch for consistency across code
reviews or onboarding new people - but all of it will be specific to
your team or company.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1430324588" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1430324588" class="expand-control">

The "why" of the change

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1430324588" class="expand-content">

People reviewing the code spend a lot less time thinking about the
change as the author. Providing the context in the description of the
change request helps them up to speed on the reasoning behind the code
changes.

**Having a clear "why" section will make the code easier to review for
people for everyone.**For people with plenty of domain knowledge, it
serves as a quick validation and they will verify if you follow what you
set out to. For people with less domain knowledge - such as recent team
members or people not familiar with the codebase - it will allow to do a
decent review. Having people who are not that familiar with the codebase
can both bring in a new perspective and it is also an efficient way to
share knowledge.

For teams using project management tools, on top of a short and clear
description, linking to the tracking task is helpful. The task often has
a lot more context, often conversations and can help the curious
reviewer dig deeper and catch up on context. The same goes for linking
other resources that can help understand the "why" deeper, such as
design documents.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-2019602566" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-2019602566" class="expand-control">

Visual support

</div>

<div id="expander-content-2019602566" class="expand-content">

For client-side changes, such as those on the web or mobile, few things
make a review easier than attaching "before" and "after" screenshots -
gifs or video recordings for changes on animations.

This is a practice that not only helps the reviewers greatly, but also
the author, who does another verification when creating the pull
request. For changes impacting the UI, without this visual aid, thorough
reviewers would have to grab the changes, build the product and run it
in their local environment to visualize the changes.

</div>

</div>

<div id="expander-1907399395" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-1907399395" class="expand-control">

Pull request guides and best practices for your team

</div>

<div id="expander-content-1907399395" class="expand-content">

My team came up with code review request best practices that we
circulated, reviewed and committed to and now use it to onboard new
members. This was a good exercise to decide what things we cared about,
as a team, and to turn best practices into*our*practices.

One thing that worked especially well was having a list of a few,
internal examples of code review requests that were deemed easy to
review. Good code review requests come in different shapes and sizes.
Having best practices helps set team-level expectations. Good

</div>

</div>

</div>

</div>

  

  

  

  

  

  

  

  

  

  

<div id="expander-530514413" class="expand-container">

<div id="expander-control-530514413" class="expand-control">

</div>

  - [Atlassian Bitbucket work with pull
    requests](https://confluence.atlassian.com/bitbucket/work-with-pull-requests-223220593.html)
  - <https://blog.pragmaticengineer.com/pull-request-or-diff-best-practices/>

</div>

</div>

  

  

</div>
